SEATTLE, Wash., July 31.â The recent
gold strike made on Fourth of July and
Ruby creeks, about 120 miles from White
Horse, will probably 'prove to be one of
the richest ever made in tha north. K.
Henderson, an old minej, has returned to
White Horse from the district. lie was
through the. Dewson : Creek stampedes
and .says thecountry is the richest he has
ever been in. He says he bilieves it will
produce more gold than any of the creeks
in the Klondike. Henderson has staked
claims on both creeks,' and has already
refused* large sums of money for his
property. On the way out Henderson
met one woman stampeder. '" He says the
Indians In the country are making for the
new diggings, as all have faith in "Daw
son Charley," the discoverer of the new
fields. Henderson made the trip from
Ruby Creek to White Horse in six days,
and says the trail 4s â¢ now in excellent
shape.
The steamer Clossett has sailed from
White Horse, for Tahkeena River with a
load of prospectors. The Vidette is also
loading a supply"- of provisions, and will
soon leave for the gold fields.- About a
dozen stampeders cam; down to White
Horse on the last trip of tho Yukoner and
are making for the creeks.
Thirteen prospectors returned to White
Horse July 26, according to the officers of
the Bteamer Humboldt, *nd all brought
stories of the wealth of the new country.
It is said that gold Is found, on tho sur
face the fu'l length of Ruby and Fourth
of July creeks. The former creek has all
been, staked. The men returning came
for tools and provisions. None of 'the
prospectors had reached bedrock.* .

MANILA. July 31.â Dominadnor Gomfz.
president of the Democratic Labor Union,
has been found guilty of contempt of
court and sentenced to imprisonment for
six months. When the union was de
clared bankrupt some time ago upon in
vestigation of its affairs by the Govern
ment, a newspaper connected with it was
suspended and placed in the hands of a
receiver. Gomez took it upon himself to
republish the paper and was promptly ar
rested upon the charge of contempt for
which he has Just been sentenced. . Â»
Gomez was arrested in May last upon a
charge of embezzlement of funds from
the labor union, the Government charging
that the money stolen was used to fur
nish arms and food to the insurgents of
Rizal Province. The union, which has a
membership of 15,000. has always been
closely allied with the National party,
and is strongly in sympathy with the in
surgents.

generally end as musicians on board the

This exhibition cf fossils, as rearranged
and made public to-day, represents the
results of thirty-three years of explora
tion and an expenditure of $250,000.
Each alcove contains a group of ar.i
' rr.ais, placed so as to 'show the evolution
in North America of the horse, the ele
phant, the mastodon and the rhinoceros.
Many cf the new and Important epeci
*nens were obtained by. the recent expedi
tion ~srat-Â«uÂ£ *t the Â«xpenc* of -William
C. Whitney.
In the alcove devoted to the evolution
of the horse the results of the Whitney
explorations in Texas, Nebraska and
Colorado are Eho-rn. Besides the an
cestral four-toed horse, representing the
beginning of the series, there are shown
the three- toed and one-toed variety. The
first named specimen was found In the
Wind River beds In Wyoming and is
about the size of a fox. Its frame is not
unlike that of the whippet, the swiftest
Cog of Its size.

ROME, July 31.â The ceremony of the
entrance of the Cardinals in conclave,
which is always given as solemn a char
acter as possible, was inaugurated this
morning-wlth the celebration of the mass
of the Holy Ghost In the Paulin Chapel,
of the Vatican. Before 1870 this mass wai
celebrated in the Chapel of the Chpir~at
St. Peter's. The Sacred College. number-
Ing sixty-two Cardinals: the diplomatic
corps, the Roman aristocracy, the Knights
of Malta and a few outsiders, especially
Invited, were present, with- a profusion of
guards, who to-day had taken oft the
mourning emblems they had been wearing
for Leo XIII.
As Is always the case at such ceremo
nials there. was so much color In the pic
ture that one felt as though .witnessing
an exhibition of a series of living pictures,
until awakened to the grand reality of the
religious ceremony by, the singing of il.e
Sistlne Choir. Cardinal Vannutelll otflcl
ated. After the Venl Sancte Spiritus had
been intoned Monseigneur Sard!, from the
puplit (which was draped In red and stood
in the choir), having before him the pon
tifical cross,, read the Latin oration. Pro
Ellgendo.Pontlflca Maximo, exhorting the
Cardinals to make a wise choice In the
task -before- them, so that the one elected
to the supreme dignity sball be a worthy
vicar of Christ on earth. Printed copies
of the oration were handed to all the Car
dinals present.

Charged With Felony.
Joseph B. D. Forde, 42 years of age,
was arrested at Reno, Nev., yesterday on
a warrant charging him with passing a
worthless draft for $73 on the Emporium
in this city. He was brought back to
San Francisco by Detective Ross Whit
aker last night.
Wound Proves Fatal.
Albert T. Henry, who sent a bullet into
his brain at his home, 119 Ninth street,
last Wednesday, died at the Emergency
Hospital yesterday from the effects of the
wound. The body was removed to the
Morgue.

The department through the free delivery

with Machen.
The contract with the manufacturers of let
ter boxes requires them to paint the boxes
yet Cupper was ordered by Machen to paint the
boxes at the factory, and the department ha*
paid him over $18,000 for this unnecessary
painting. Cupper paid Long a part of thla
money, who divided It with Machen.
Cupper. Long and Machen have been in
dicted for conspiracy to defraud the Govern
ment.

About fifteen feet deep at that point.
this afternoon, precipitating more
than one hundred people forty feet into

all are accounted for. Many . struck on
two small boathouses moored to a pier
of the bridge immediately under the spot
where It gave way. About twenty-five
were injured either by striking on the
boathouses or by falling timbers. Many
people fell from the roofs of the * boat
houses into the water, but dozens of
Email boats and launches in the vicinity
in an incredibly short time commenced to
pick them up.
Thousands of people had gathered on
the Morrison and Madison street bridges
and along the docks to watch Clarence
Lutz, an armless man, swim the river,
which is about three-eighths of a mile
in width at that point.**
Lutz started" from a boathouse on the
west, side of the river and swam to the
boathouse of the Portland Rowing Club,
moored on the east side. Just above the
Morrison street bridge. As he was climb.
ing out of the water the crowd rushed to
the south edge of the bridge in order to
get a good view. A section of the pas
senger walk gave way under the heavy
weight and the crowding, struggling mass
of humanity was carried down a distance
of forty feet â¢

The American Indian, accustomed from
time immemorial to go bareheaded in all
kinds of weather, is never troubled with
falling hair or baldness.

breeding of infinitesimal perms, which dig
into the scalp and thrive on the sap of
the hair root.

HUNTINGTON. W. Va.. July 3L-Ches
ape&ke and Ohio express train No. 1 was
held up by a mob of 2D0 men near Clifton
Forge -last . night and a desperate but
unsuccessful effort was made to take two
negro prisoners from the train. Over a
hundred shots were fired by the mob. The
engineer, being left unguarded, started
the train, which ran away from the mob.
The negroes for whom the train was
held up are from Lynchburg, Va., and are
believed to be the men who shot the
white men a few days ago.

This story was the cause of the rush
orders sent over this .county and parts
of others. All roads in the vicinity of
Lotus were closely guarded last night.
Two hundred men eagerly awaited the
appearance of dawn as the rumor was
that five convicts were surrounded. At
4 o'clock this morning the town was
astir and mounted men were hurrying
hither and thither. Before sunrise a
small- army in -various detachments
marched on the Webber Creek district
That was all there was to it. except sun
burn, aching joints and profanity.
The 'militiamen had a vigorous wrari
gle with men -of the Sheriffs posse*
last night over thefruitlessness of the
chase and spent to-day resting in the
shade at Lotus. No one has any defin
ite ideas as to the whereabouts of the
escaped felons. Theories are as num
erous as manzanita bushes. \ .:/
Jasper Jurgens has asserted that he
heard about ten shots this morning two
miles from the postoffice. Sheriff.Bos
quit heard the â¢ same rifle reports and.
investigated the . whole neighborhood
for the supposed conflict without result.-
It is thought j that one of the Salmon
Falls detachments fired the shots,. After
thoroughly canvassing the situation the
different posses returned to Lotus.

roy departed late this afternoon in a
buggy for Georgetown. The veteran
officers plan to scout through Black
Rock arid Red Rock canvons and
Greenwood Creeks for several days.

Five members of the militia company
have arrived here and they are almost
worn out with travel. They say that it Is
the opinion among the posses that the
convicts have headed for the north to
ward Placer County and the Rubicon
"country.
The militia will return to Placerville
late to-night. Sheriff Bosqutt of this
county is due here to-night with his
posse. There has been no one to direct
the manhunters, and there has been con
siderable jealousy among the various
posses.
The exclusive story In The Call to-day
that the convicts had planned before the
prison break to take the "old robbers'
trail" has caused considerable comment
here.. Every one is now satisfied that this
information is correct and proven so by
the failure of the posses to find any trace
of the convicts around Webber Creek,
Pine Hill. Lotus or Coloma.
JEALOUSY IN POSSES.
"This information has been in possession
of the Georgetown officers for the past
three days and they investigated before
calling for assistance. Now that they feel
almost sure that some of the convicts are
heading for the Rubicon country aid has
been called for.
.^Warren Green, a merchant of George
town, telephoned here this evening that
the story of school children regarding
'some armed men near the Seven-mile
House last Wednesday evening has been
verified. Constable Steermon of George
town has been out on the trail since yes
terday and he went up to the Seven-mile
House and ascertained that six seven
men had passed there. The tracks led
from Coloma Canyon along the "old
road" to Taylor's mine, past the old Al
pine mine" and then turned into the dense
brush. These tracks found to-day clearly
indicate that a party of men Is heading
northward to the old volcano, which is on
the "old robbers' trail," leading to the
Rubfcon country.
Foreman - Tolman of the Darling mine,
Â¦which is near the Alpine mine, was asked
to-day over the telephone by Deputy
Sheriff Bosquit of Placerville if he had
heard of any of the convicts being in that
vicinity. Talman replied that on Tuesday
evening two armed men stopped him as
he was driving on the road and asked him
if he "had seen any officers. He told them
that he had not and the men then went
off into the dense brush.
....' Folsom Prison laet Monday have
.headed over the "old robbers' trail" lead
ing to the Rubicon country. The chase
made by the sheriffs and . militia, around
Lotus, Cool, Coloma, Webber Creek and
Pine Hill has availed nothing. The gen
eral belief is that the convicts headed for
the north after the battle with the posses
at Pilot Hill last Monday night.
Information was received to-day that
â¢six j or eight men have gone northward
over the "old robbers' trail" and the
â¢posses are now heading In that direction.
To-night^ all the roads and streams in the
vicinity 'of Georgetown are being pa
trolled and xÂ»oEses are to concentrate in
that region by to-morrow morning. The
driver of the stage from Georgetown to
Placerville distinctly saw the trails of six
or eight men last Tuesday morning some
diEtance from Georgetown. '

THE SAN FEANCiSCO CALL, SATURDAY,; AIZGUST 1, Wv3.
PLACER VILLE, July 31. â A telephone message received from Lotus to-night says that a posse of * Folsom prison guards fired on .a man supposed to be the
colored convict, Seavis, at Orelli's ranch, about two "miles from Lotus. The guards had driven up in a wagon to the* ranch shortly before dusk, and they were
standing on-the porchof the house, when they saw a man coming up the road in the direction of the house. When the man saw the guardshe started to go
back. The guards went after him and called on him to halt. He failed to do so and the posse opened fire on him just as he disappeared in the dense brush. The
guards will patrol: the road all night, as it is clear and the moon is bright, and further help is to be sent" from Lotus as soon as dawn, arrives.

BAKERSFIELD, July 31.â Word was
received here" Jate '. this afternoon of a
shocking murder at Kernville, a mountain
town thirty miles from the rallrpad. The
crime, which is the climax pf a series pf
minpr acts pf lawlessness during the past
week, was cpmmitted last night, when
some unknown person killed in cold blood
a negro by the name of Williams, who
was employed on the power, plant of the
Huntlngton-Hellman syndicate : at that
point. The assailant stole up on his vic
tim and struck him from behind with a

The .announcement .was made to
night that â¢ Gpvernpr Peabody was
this "afternoon waited upon, by.-, a
committee from, th* union miners who
were driven out of Idaho Springs by 500

Flder their request, for protection from

Leading citizens of Idaho Springs have
taken up the detective work and axe con
stantly developing damaging evidence
against the miners' union. As a conse
quence members are constantly being or
dered out of town and all trains and ve
hicles arriving are closely, watched to see
that the evicted men do not return and
to prevent officers of the Western Fed
eration of Miners entering camp.
It has developed that Philip Fine, the
dynamiter who was found dead after
Tuesday night's explosion that wrecked
the Sun -and Moon works, was not shot
by the- watchman; bu( was killed by some
missile, which exploded while he w-as car
rying it. %
DENVER, July SI.â Evidence that the

cated in the Idaho Springs dynamite plot
has been found on the perspn of II. E.
Gregory of the Georgetown union, now
under arrest for threats to blow up the

his papers- was a letter from Chandler,
financial, secretary of the Idaho Springs
union, offering union funds and aid for
the dynamiting of the plant. Other evi
dence Incriminating union officers and
many members has been obtained by
"sweating" Gregory and Bate, the former
president of the body, now in Jail.

the present trial Is declare* to be a suc
cess.

authorized in 1S97. So far it has cost
the Government $33,000. *

leges buildings In San Frandscp, 0 as well as to

.raittee, of which Professor Putnam, President

Is likely to result in plans for the permanent
housing of the museum. Mrs. Hearst is Inter
ested in the collection, for which she has spent
thousands L of dollars, and. ehe may decide to
build a museum on the campus.

partment of anthropology in the American Mui
seum of Natural History of New, York. He has
observed that the Hearst Museum is abreast

tivity. The Hearst collection Includes antiqui
